        <title>The impact of genome evolution on the allotetraploid Nicotiana rustica – an intriguing story of enhanced alkaloid production | BMC Genomics | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Nicotiana rustica (Aztec tobacco), like common tobacco (Nicotiana tabacum), is an allotetraploid formed through a recent hybridization event; however, it originated from completely different progenitor species. Here, we report the comparative genome analysis of wild type N. rustica (5 Gb; 2n&#8201;=&#8201;4x&#8201;=&#8201;48) with its three putative diploid progenitors (2.3&#8211;3 Gb; 2n&#8201;=&#8201;2x =24), Nicotiana undulata, Nicotiana paniculata and Nicotiana knightiana. In total, 41% of N. rustica genome originated from the paternal donor (N. undulata), while 59% originated from the maternal donor (N. paniculata/N. knightiana). Chloroplast genome and gene analyses indicated that N. knightiana is more closely related to N. rustica than N. paniculata. Gene clustering revealed 14,623 ortholog groups common to other Nicotiana species and 207 unique to N. rustica. Genome sequence analysis indicated that N. knightiana is more closely related to N. rustica than N. paniculata, and that the higher nicotine content of N. rustica leaves is the result of the progenitor genomes combination and of a more active transport of nicotine to the shoot. The availability of four new Nicotiana genome sequences provide insights into how speciation impacts plant metabolism, and in particular alkaloid transport and accumulation, and will contribute to better understanding the evolution of Nicotiana species."/>

            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );

        <meta property="og:url" content="https://bmcgenomics.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5"/>
        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="BMC Genomics"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="The impact of genome evolution on the allotetraploid Nicotiana rustica – an intriguing story of enhanced alkaloid production"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Nicotiana rustica (Aztec tobacco), like common tobacco (Nicotiana tabacum), is an allotetraploid formed through a recent hybridization event; however, it originated from completely different progenitor species. Here, we report the comparative genome analysis of wild type N. rustica (5 Gb; 2n &#x3D; 4x &#x3D; 48) with its three putative diploid progenitors (2.3–3 Gb; 2n &#x3D; 2x &#x3D;24), Nicotiana undulata, Nicotiana paniculata and Nicotiana knightiana. In total, 41% of N. rustica genome originated from the paternal donor (N. undulata), while 59% originated from the maternal donor (N. paniculata/N. knightiana). Chloroplast genome and gene analyses indicated that N. knightiana is more closely related to N. rustica than N. paniculata. Gene clustering revealed 14,623 ortholog groups common to other Nicotiana species and 207 unique to N. rustica. Genome sequence analysis indicated that N. knightiana is more closely related to N. rustica than N. paniculata, and that the higher nicotine content of N. rustica leaves is the result of the progenitor genomes combination and of a more active transport of nicotine to the shoot. The availability of four new Nicotiana genome sequences provide insights into how speciation impacts plant metabolism, and in particular alkaloid transport and accumulation, and will contribute to better understanding the evolution of Nicotiana species."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/12864.jpg"/>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p><i>Nicotiana rustica</i> (Aztec tobacco), like common tobacco (<i>Nicotiana tabacum</i>), is an allotetraploid formed through a recent hybridization event; however, it originated from completely different progenitor species. Here, we report the comparative genome analysis of wild type <i>N. rustica</i> (5 Gb; 2n = 4x = 48) with its three putative diploid progenitors (2.3–3 Gb; 2n = 2x =24), <i>Nicotiana undulata</i>, <i>Nicotiana paniculata</i> and <i>Nicotiana knightiana</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p>In total, 41% of <i>N. rustica</i> genome originated from the paternal donor (<i>N. undulata</i>), while 59% originated from the maternal donor (<i>N. paniculata</i>/<i>N. knightiana</i>). Chloroplast genome and gene analyses indicated that <i>N. knightiana</i> is more closely related to <i>N. rustica</i> than <i>N. paniculata</i>. Gene clustering revealed 14,623 ortholog groups common to other Nicotiana species and 207 unique to <i>N. rustica</i>. Genome sequence analysis indicated that <i>N. knightiana</i> is more closely related to <i>N. rustica</i> than <i>N. paniculata</i>, and that the higher nicotine content of <i>N. rustica</i> leaves is the result of the progenitor genomes combination and of a more active transport of nicotine to the shoot.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>The availability of four new Nicotiana genome sequences provide insights into how speciation impacts plant metabolism, and in particular alkaloid transport and accumulation, and will contribute to better understanding the evolution of Nicotiana species.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>While <i>Nicotiana tabacum</i> is the most notable species from the Nicotiana genus, various other Nicotiana species are cultivated as crops, grown as ornamental garden plants or used as model organisms in research. Aztec or Indian tobacco, <i>Nicotiana rustica</i>, is suspected to be the original tobacco species that was brought from the Americas to Europe. Known as “<i>mapacho</i>”, it was considered sacred and medicinal by Amazonian shamans. Even though, in terms of production, it has been superseded in the last century by its relative <i>N. tabacum</i>, Aztec tobacco is still cultivated in South America, Turkey, Russia and Vietnam, mostly owing to its resilience to adverse climatic conditions.</p><p>Morphologically, <i>N. rustica</i> is recognized for its characteristic yellow flowers that form a tube (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S1) and leaves that are covered with trichomes rich in secondary metabolites, including nicotine, nornicotine, anatabine and anabasine [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Sisson VA, Severson RF. Alkaloid composition of the Nicotiana species. Beitr Zur Tab Int. 1990;14:327–39." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d16228e657">1</a>]. The high leaf concentration of nicotine (5–15% dry leaf weight) prompted its use in the production of nicotine-based pesticides, nicotine sulfate and nicotinic acid. Because of even higher levels of citric acid (15–20% of dry leaf weight), the leaves of <i>N. rustica</i> are an excellent source of this important metabolite [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Blinova KF, Borisova NA, Gortinskij GB. Botanico-pharmacological dictionary (Ботанико-фармакогностический словарь). Moscow: Higher School (Высшая школа; 1990." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d16228e663">2</a>]. Scientific reports describing active accumulation of nicotine in <i>N. rustica</i> compared to <i>N. tabacum</i> are rather scarce [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Reznicek AA, Voss EG, Walters BS. Michigan Flora Online. 2011. &#xA;                    https://michiganflora.net/species.aspx?id=2701&#xA;                    &#xA;                  . Accessed 15 Aug 2018." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d16228e673">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Buchanan R. A Short History of Tobacco: the Most Provocative Herb. Herb Companion. 1994;7:34–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d16228e676">4</a>]. Interestingly, on the opposite to the nicotine level <i>N. rustica</i> exhibits a lower leaf versus root cadmium ratio compared to <i>N. tabacum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wagner GJ, Yeargan R. Variation in cadmium accumulation potential and tissue distribution of cadmium in tobacco. Plant Physiol. 1986;82:274–9." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d16228e686">5</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bovet L, Rossi L, Lugon-Moulin N. Cadmium partitioning and gene expression studies in Nicotiana tabacum and Nicotiana rustica. Physiol Plant. 2006;128:466–75." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d16228e689">6</a>]. As root is both involved in nicotine synthesis, cadmium uptake and shoot translocation, root pathways may have interconnection, Cd being reported to have toxic properties regarding plant nutrition [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Muradoglu F, Gundogdu M, Ercisli S, Encu T, Balta F, Jaafar H, et al. Cadmium toxicity affects chlorophyll a and b content, antioxidant enzyme activities and mineral nutrient accumulation in strawberry. Biol Res. 2015;48:11." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d16228e692">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Carvalho Bertoli A, Gabriel Cannata M, Carvalho R, Ribeiro Bastos AR, Puggina Freitas M, dos Santos Augusto A. Lycopersicon esculentum submitted to Cd-stressful conditions in nutrition solution: nutrient contents and translocation. Ecotoxicol Environ Saf. 2012;86:176–81." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d16228e695">8</a>]. On the side of Cd, although Zn accumulation, but not Fe and Mn, may vary between the two species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bovet L, Rossi L, Lugon-Moulin N. Cadmium partitioning and gene expression studies in Nicotiana tabacum and Nicotiana rustica. Physiol Plant. 2006;128:466–75." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d16228e698">6</a>], no reports mention yet variation of K and Na.</p><p>Within the Solanaceae family, the genomes of the Nicotiana species are peculiar. First, they have relatively large genomes that are similar in size to those of Capsicum species and two to three times larger than those of Solanum and Petunia species. Second, the Nicotiana genus contains many species that can be used to study the evolution of polyploidy in plants. Although the majority of the more than 70 Nicotiana species is diploid with <i>n</i> = 12, five sections of the Nicotiana family (<i>Nicotiana</i>, <i>Polydicliae</i>, <i>Repandae</i>, <i>Undulatae</i> and <i>Rusticae</i>) include allopolyploid species with <i>n</i> = 24 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Leitch IJ, Hanson L, Lim KY, Kovarik A, Chase MW, Clarkson JJ, et al. The ups and downs of genome size evolution in polyploid species of Nicotiana (Solanaceae). Ann Bot. 2008;101:805–14." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d16228e726">9</a>]. Molecular clock analyses estimate the dates of polyploidization events as ranging from less than 0.2 million years ago (<i>Nicotiana arentsii</i>, <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. tabacum</i>) to more than 10 million years ago (a single polyploidization event from which sect. <i>Suaveolentes</i> is descended) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Leitch IJ, Hanson L, Lim KY, Kovarik A, Chase MW, Clarkson JJ, et al. The ups and downs of genome size evolution in polyploid species of Nicotiana (Solanaceae). Ann Bot. 2008;101:805–14." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d16228e742">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Clarkson JJ, Knapp S, Garcia VF, Olmstead RG, Leitch AR, Chase MW. Phylogenetic relationships in Nicotiana (Solanaceae) inferred from multiple plastid DNA regions. Mol Phylogenet Evol. 2004;33:75–90." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d16228e742_1">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Clarkson JJ, Lim KY, Kovarik A, Chase MW, Knapp S, Leitch AR. Long-term genome diploidization in allopolyploid Nicotiana section Repandae (Solanaceae). New Phytol. 2005;168:241–52." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d16228e745">11</a>]. To date, only the progenitor species of <i>N. tabacum</i> (<i>Nicotiana sylvestris</i> and <i>Nicotiana tomentosiformis</i>) have been well characterized [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Sierro N, Battey JND, Ouadi S, Bovet L, Goepfert S, Bakaher N, et al. Reference genomes and transcriptomes of Nicotiana sylvestris and Nicotiana tomentosiformis. Genome Biol. 2013;14:R60." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d16228e758">12</a>], and the presence of previously identified species-specific translocations in <i>N. tabacum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Leitch IJ, Bennett MD. Polyploidy in angiosperms. Trends Plant Sci. 1997;2:470–6." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d16228e764">13</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Bindler G, Plieske J, Bakaher N, Gunduz I, Ivanov N, Van der Hoeven R, et al. A high density genetic map of tobacco (Nicotiana tabacum L.) obtained from large scale microsatellite marker development. Theor Appl Genet. 2011;123:219–30." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d16228e767">14</a>] have been confirmed [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Sierro N, Battey JND, Ouadi S, Bakaher N, Bovet L, Willig A, et al. The tobacco genome sequence and its comparison with those of tomato and potato. Nat Commun. 2014;5:3833." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d16228e770">15</a>].</p><p>Based on morphology, cytology and artificial hybridization experiments, Goodspeed [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Goodspeed TH. The genus Nicotiana. Chron Bot. 1954;16:1–536." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d16228e776">16</a>] proposed the likely progenitors of the polyploid species of the Nicotiana genus. The origins of 15 allopolyploid <i>Nicotiana</i> species were explored by genomic in situ hybridization (GISH) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Chase MW, Knapp S, Cox AV, Clarkson JJ, Butsko Y, Joseph J, et al. Molecular systematics, GISH and the origin of hybrid taxa in Nicotiana (Solanaceae). Ann Bot. 2003;92:107–27." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d16228e782">17</a>], and fluorescently labeled DNA probes from the genomes of <i>N. undulata</i> and <i>N. paniculata</i> marked the complimentary chromosomes of <i>N. rustica,</i> confirming Goodspeed's hypothesis of their parental relationships. Lim et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Lim KY, Matyasek R, Kovarik A, Leitch AR. Genome evolution in allotetraploid Nicotiana. Biol J Linn Soc. 2004;82:599–606." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d16228e795">18</a>] investigated genome evolution in three natural allopolyploid species (<i>N. arentsii</i>, <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. tabacum</i>) using GISH and fluorescent in situ hybridization. Unlike in <i>N. tabacum</i> cultivars, no intergenomic translocations were observed in <i>N. rustica</i>; thus, the probes from <i>N. undulata</i> and <i>N. paniculata</i> have been exclusively mapped to the U- and P-genomes, respectively, of <i>N. rustica</i>.</p><p>Using complementary PCR-based techniques and the internal transcribed spacer sequences of nuclear ribosomal DNA [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Chase MW, Knapp S, Cox AV, Clarkson JJ, Butsko Y, Joseph J, et al. Molecular systematics, GISH and the origin of hybrid taxa in Nicotiana (Solanaceae). Ann Bot. 2003;92:107–27." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d16228e827">17</a>] and the chloroplast gene <i>matK</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Aoki S, Ito M. Molecular phylogeny of Nicotiana (Solanaceae) based on the nucleotide sequence of the matK gene. Plant Biol. 2000;2:316–24." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d16228e833">19</a>], the parental relationships of species were assessed. They provided further evidence that either <i>N. knightiana</i> or <i>N. paniculata</i> could be the maternal donor, and both techniques identified <i>N. undulata</i> as the paternal donor. Interestingly, <i>N. undulata</i> serves as a maternal donor in the hybridization with <i>Nicotiana wigandioides</i>, to form the allotetraploid <i>N. arentsii</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Chase MW, Knapp S, Cox AV, Clarkson JJ, Butsko Y, Joseph J, et al. Molecular systematics, GISH and the origin of hybrid taxa in Nicotiana (Solanaceae). Ann Bot. 2003;92:107–27." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d16228e855">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Aoki S, Ito M. Molecular phylogeny of Nicotiana (Solanaceae) based on the nucleotide sequence of the matK gene. Plant Biol. 2000;2:316–24." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d16228e858">19</a>].</p><p>Unfortunately, <i>N. knightiana</i> was not investigated using GISH. Based on a screen of 75 Nicotiana species with several chloroplast genes, Clarkson et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Clarkson JJ, Knapp S, Garcia VF, Olmstead RG, Leitch AR, Chase MW. Phylogenetic relationships in Nicotiana (Solanaceae) inferred from multiple plastid DNA regions. Mol Phylogenet Evol. 2004;33:75–90." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d16228e867">10</a>] established that <i>N. knightiana</i> is genetically closer than <i>N. paniculata</i> (one vs five substitutions) to <i>N. rustica</i>. Nevertheless, this still suggested that a common ancestor of both <i>N. knightiana</i> and <i>N. paniculata</i> served as the maternal donor to <i>N. rustica</i>. Thus, to date, the identity of the progenitor species from section Paniculatae that is the maternal donor to the <i>N. rustica</i> genome remains unclear. Our analysis of the chloroplast genomes from all four species shed light on this topic.</p><p>The genomes of <i>Nicotiana benthamiana</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Naim F, Nakasugi K, Crowhurst RN, Hilario E, Zwart AB, Hellens RP, et al. Advanced engineering of lipid metabolism in Nicotiana benthamiana using a draft genome and the V2 viral silencing-suppressor protein. PLoS One. 2012;7:e52717." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d16228e898">20</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Bombarely A, Rosli HG, Vrebalov J, Moffett P, Mueller L, Martin G. A draft genome sequence of Nicotiana benthamiana to enhance molecular plant-microbe biology research. Mol Plant-Microbe Interact. 2012;25:1523–30." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d16228e901">21</a>], <i>N. otophora</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Sierro N, Battey JND, Ouadi S, Bakaher N, Bovet L, Willig A, et al. The tobacco genome sequence and its comparison with those of tomato and potato. Nat Commun. 2014;5:3833." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d16228e907">15</a>], <i>N. sylvestris</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Sierro N, Battey JND, Ouadi S, Bovet L, Goepfert S, Bakaher N, et al. Reference genomes and transcriptomes of Nicotiana sylvestris and Nicotiana tomentosiformis. Genome Biol. 2013;14:R60." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d16228e914">12</a>], <i>N. tabacum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Sierro N, Battey JND, Ouadi S, Bakaher N, Bovet L, Willig A, et al. The tobacco genome sequence and its comparison with those of tomato and potato. Nat Commun. 2014;5:3833." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d16228e920">15</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Edwards KD, Fernandez-Pozo N, Drake-Stowe K, Humphry M, Evans AD, Bombarely A, et al. A reference genome for Nicotiana tabacum enables map-based cloning of homeologous loci implicated in nitrogen utilization efficiency. BMC Genomics. 2017;18:448." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d16228e923">22</a>], <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Sierro N, Battey JND, Ouadi S, Bovet L, Goepfert S, Bakaher N, et al. Reference genomes and transcriptomes of Nicotiana sylvestris and Nicotiana tomentosiformis. Genome Biol. 2013;14:R60." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d16228e930">12</a>], <i>N. attenuata</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Xu S, Brockmoller T, Navarro-Quezada A, Kuhl H, Gase K, Ling Z, et al. Wild tobacco genomes reveal the evolution of nicotine biosynthesis. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2017;114:6133–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d16228e936">23</a>] and <i>N. obtusifolia</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Xu S, Brockmoller T, Navarro-Quezada A, Kuhl H, Gase K, Ling Z, et al. Wild tobacco genomes reveal the evolution of nicotine biosynthesis. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A. 2017;114:6133–8." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d16228e942">23</a>] have been sequenced and draft assemblies published, enabling genome-based evolutionary studies of Nicotiana species. With the exception of <i>N. benthamiana</i>, <i>N. attenuata</i> and <i>N. obtusifolia</i>, all of the published Nicotiana genomes are closely related to <i>N. tabacum</i>. Here, we present the genomes and transcriptomes of <i>N. rustica</i> and its putative ancestral species, <i>N. undulata</i>, <i>N. paniculata</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i>. We elucidate the mechanism behind the upregulated nicotine production in these plants and provide insights into the metabolic and genomic differences in comparison with <i>N. tabacum</i> and its ancestors, <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, also focusing on the accumulation of essential and non-essential elements as well as major free amino acids, no studies being available yet.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing, assembly and annotation</h3><p>We sequenced the genomes of <i>N. rustica</i> and its potential progenitors, <i>N. undulata</i>, <i>N. paniculata</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i>, using reads from Illumina HiSeq2500 and Pacific Biosciences RSII sequencers and performed de novo genome assemblies. The estimations of their genome sizes based on 31-k-mer depth distributions of raw sequencing reads were 4.99 Gb for <i>N. rustica</i>, 2.18 Gb for <i>N. undulata</i>, 3.26 Gb for <i>N. paniculata</i> and 3.12 Gb for <i>N. knightiana</i>, which are consistent with the sizes (5.181, 2.362, 2.880 and 3.090 Gb, respectively) reported in the KEW c-DNA database. Based on these numbers, the genome of <i>N. rustica</i> was reduced by 5.9 or 8.3% compared with the sum of the genome sizes from <i>N. undulata</i> and either <i>N. paniculata</i> or <i>N. knightiana</i>, respectively. This reduction corresponds to the upper bound proposed by Leitch et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Leitch IJ, Hanson L, Lim KY, Kovarik A, Chase MW, Clarkson JJ, et al. The ups and downs of genome size evolution in polyploid species of Nicotiana (Solanaceae). Ann Bot. 2008;101:805–14." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d16228e1030">9</a>] and is similar to the reduction in genome size reported for <i>N. tabacum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Sierro N, Battey JND, Ouadi S, Bakaher N, Bovet L, Willig A, et al. The tobacco genome sequence and its comparison with those of tomato and potato. Nat Commun. 2014;5:3833." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d16228e1036">15</a>].</p><p>The assembled genome sequences consisted of 117,559 to 246,567 scaffolds, covering from 67.2 to 89.2%, respectively, of the estimated genomes. The N50 lengths were between 52.8 and 84.6 kb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Tab1">1</a>). The comparatively low k-mer-based genome coverage of <i>N. paniculata</i> (67.2%) results from the overestimation of its genome size when using 31-k-mers. While the estimation methods produced similar genome sizes for the other species, this was not the case for <i>N. paniculata</i>, for which flow cytometric measurements resulted in a smaller estimated genome size.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Nicotiana genome assembly metrics</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome repeat contents</h3><p>An analysis of the assembled genomes organization showed that 22 to 25% of the sequenced genomes consisted of genes and regulatory sequences (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Tab1">1</a>). The 75 to 78% of the genomes identified as repeats consisted, to a large extent, of long terminal repeat elements (~ 45% of the genome assemblies). The proportions of each type of repeat element found in <i>N. rustica</i>, <i>N. undulata</i>, <i>N. knightiana</i> and <i>N. paniculata</i> were very similar. They also correspond to those previously observed in <i>N. tabacum</i> and its ancestors [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Sierro N, Battey JND, Ouadi S, Bovet L, Goepfert S, Bakaher N, et al. Reference genomes and transcriptomes of Nicotiana sylvestris and Nicotiana tomentosiformis. Genome Biol. 2013;14:R60." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d16228e1686">12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Sierro N, Battey JND, Ouadi S, Bakaher N, Bovet L, Willig A, et al. The tobacco genome sequence and its comparison with those of tomato and potato. Nat Commun. 2014;5:3833." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d16228e1689">15</a>].</p><p>Lim et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Lim KY, Matyasek R, Kovarik A, Leitch AR. Genome evolution in allotetraploid Nicotiana. Biol J Linn Soc. 2004;82:599–606." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d16228e1695">18</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Lim KY, Matyasek R, Kovarik A, Fulnecek J, Leitch AR. Molecular cytogenetics and tandem repeat sequence evolution in the allopolyploid Nicotiana rustica compared with diploid progenitors N. paniculata and N. undulata. Cytogenet Genome Res. 2005;109:298–309." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d16228e1698">24</a>] used GISH and fluorescent in situ hybridization to detect rearrangements and at least 10-fold reductions in the NPAMBO repeat content within the P-genome of <i>N. rustica</i> compared within that of <i>N. paniculata</i>. Based on the draft genome assemblies, no such reduction in the NPAMBO repeat element was observed (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S1).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome assembly completeness</h3><p>The completeness of the genomes was assessed using Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO) with the embryophyta plant dataset, which consists of 1440 universal single-copy orthologs [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Simao FA, Waterhouse RM, Ioannidis P, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics. 2015;31:3210–2." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d16228e1718">25</a>]. Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#MOESM3">3</a>: Figure S2 shows the percentage of these universal single-copy orthologs that were identified as complete, duplicated, fragmented and missing in Nicotiana genomes, other Solanacea genomes, <i>Vitis vinifera</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>.</p><p>Most diploid Nicotiana species contain approximately 95% complete universal single-copy orthologs (approximately 90% as single copies and 5% as duplicates), similar to other diploid Solanacea species and <i>A. thaliana</i>. <i>N. otophora</i> is the only exception, with approximately 75% single copied and 5% duplicated complete universal single-copy orthologs. It also contains approximately 10% fragmented universal single-copy orthologs, which is higher than in the other genomes.</p><p>The tetraploid Nicotiana species also contain approximately 95% complete universal single-copy orthologs. However, the proportions of single copies and duplicates are very different, with only approximately 30% of single copies complete universal single-copy orthologs being contained in the recent tetraploids <i>N. tabacum</i> and <i>N. rustica</i>, and approximately 50% in the more ancient tetraploid <i>N. benthamiana</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Parental origins based on raw sequencing data</h3><p>The 31-k-mers present in the raw sequencing reads of <i>N. rustica</i>, <i>N. undulata</i>, <i>N. paniculata</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i> between 5 and 250 times were compared to evaluate the overlap among the four species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Fig1">1</a>a). Approximately 50% of them are unique to one species and only 2% are common to all four species; 18% are common to <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. undulata</i>, and 26% to <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. paniculata</i> and/or <i>N. knightiana</i> (12% with both species, 8% with <i>N. knightiana</i> and 6% with <i>N. paniculata</i>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-018-5241-5/MediaObjects/12864_2018_5241_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-018-5241-5/MediaObjects/12864_2018_5241_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="249" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Parental origins of <i>N. rustica</i> based on the 31-k-mer analysis. <b>a</b> 31-k-mers from <i>N. rustica</i> and its progenitors; <b>b</b> 31-k-mers from <i>N. rustica</i> only</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Of the 31-k-mers found in the <i>N. rustica</i> raw reads (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Fig1">1</a>b), 25% are unique to <i>N. rustica</i>, 29% are shared with <i>N. undulata</i>, and 41% with <i>N. paniculata</i> and/or <i>N. knightiana</i>.</p><p>The proportion of 31-k-mers found in one of the two ancestors indicates that 41.4% of the <i>N. rustica</i> genome originated from <i>N. undulata</i> and 58.6% from <i>N. paniculata</i> and/or <i>N. knightiana</i>, which is consistent with the genome contributions of each ancestor to the hybridization (40.6 and 59.4%, respectively, calculated using predicted genome sizes of 2.18 Gb for <i>N. undulata</i>, 3.19 Gb for <i>N. paniculata</i>/<i>N. knightiana</i> (average of the predicted 3.12 Gb and 3.26 Gb genome sizes), and 5.37 Gb for <i>N. rustica</i> at the time of hybridization). Based on an estimated <i>N. rustica</i> genome of 4.99 Gb, the 41.4% contributed by <i>N. undulata</i> accounted for 2.07 Gb (0.11 Gb downsizing from the estimated <i>N. undulata</i> genome size), and <i>N. paniculata</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i> accounted for the remaining 2.92 Gb (0.27 Gb downsizing from the average of the estimated <i>N. paniculata</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i> genome sizes).</p><p>In comparison, in <i>N. tabacum</i>, the 31-k-mer analysis showed that 40.3% of the genome originated from <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> and 59.7% from <i>N. sylvestris</i>, whereas the estimated genome sizes of the ancestors indicated contributions to the hybridization of 46.2 and 53.8%, respectively, calculated with genome sizes of 2.22 Gb for <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> and 2.59 Gb for <i>N. sylvestris</i>, and 4.81 Gb for <i>N. tabacum</i> at the time of hybridization<i>.</i> Based on an estimated <i>N. tabacum</i> genome size of 4.41 Gb [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Sierro N, Battey JND, Ouadi S, Bakaher N, Bovet L, Willig A, et al. The tobacco genome sequence and its comparison with those of tomato and potato. Nat Commun. 2014;5:3833." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d16228e1930">15</a>], the 40.3% contributed by <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> accounted for 1.78 Gb (0.44 Gb downsizing from the estimated <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> genome size), and <i>N. sylvestris</i> accounted for the remaining 2.63 Gb (0.04 Gb upsizing from the estimated <i>N. sylvestris</i> genome size) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Sierro N, Battey JND, Ouadi S, Bovet L, Goepfert S, Bakaher N, et al. Reference genomes and transcriptomes of Nicotiana sylvestris and Nicotiana tomentosiformis. Genome Biol. 2013;14:R60." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d16228e1946">12</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Maternal parent of <i>N. rustica</i> based on a chloroplast genome analysis</h3><p>The maternal parent of the tetraploid <i>N. rustica</i> was identified as being the ancestor of <i>N. paniculata</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i> by mapping the short sequencing reads from <i>N. rustica</i> and its ancestors to the chloroplast genome of <i>N. tabacum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Yukawa M, Tsudzuki T, Sugiura M. The chloroplast genome of Nicotiana sylvestris and Nicotiana tomentosiformis: complete sequencing confirms that the Nicotiana sylvestris progenitor is the maternal genome donor of Nicotiana tabacum. Mol Gen Genomics. 2006;275:367–73." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d16228e1978">26</a>] and by assessing the number of single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) shared between <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. undulata</i>, and between <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. paniculata</i> and/or <i>N. knightiana</i>. Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#MOESM4">4</a>: Figure S3 shows the overlap of the chloroplast genomes from the four Nicotiana species based on the number of common SNPs. We observed 336 SNPs unique to <i>N. undulata</i>, 8 shared between <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. undulata</i>, 303 SNPs shared by <i>N. rustica</i>, <i>N. paniculata</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i>, 7 by <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. paniculata</i>, 17 by <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i> and 11 by <i>N. paniculata</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i>. The chloroplast genome of <i>N. knightiana</i> appears to be closer than that of <i>N. paniculata</i> to the <i>N. rustica</i> chloroplast genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Transcriptomics analysis</h3><p>For each species, samples were taken from various organs under different conditions and at different time points. For <i>N. rustica</i>, two sets of transcriptomes were generated, one including eight tissues at a single time point, (flower bud, mature flower, mature capsule, lower leaf, middle leaf, upper leaf, stem and root) and the other one containing only root, upper and lower leaves and flower. For the putative progenitors, only the four-tissue set was generated. Expressed gene families were assigned using OrthoMCL. The gene families from the four-tissue set were used to ensure the comparability of datasets. As shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Fig2">2</a>, there is a common core of 14,623 ortholog groups that is shared by all species. As evidenced by the 207 ortholog groups specific to <i>N. rustica</i>, it has experienced only a minor divergence, as have the pseudo-progenitor species, each having fewer than 62 exclusive ortholog groups. <i>N. rustica</i> shares 1037 ortholog groups exclusively with <i>N. undulata</i>. Because <i>N. paniculata</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i> have diverged from the most recent common ancestor, the share of exclusive common orthologs is split between the two pseudo-progenitors, and <i>N. paniculata</i> appears closer to <i>N. rustica</i> (711) than <i>N. knightiana</i> (470 groups).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-018-5241-5/MediaObjects/12864_2018_5241_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-018-5241-5/MediaObjects/12864_2018_5241_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="389" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Numbers of OrthoMCL clusters of orthologous proteins based on RNA sequencing data</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>No potential regulators or genes of the nicotine biosynthesis pathway were identified in clusters of orthologous genes that are specific to either <i>N. tabacum</i> or <i>N. rustica</i> based on annotation assigned by blastp searches against TAIR and ITAG proteins, as well as against key regulators and genes of the nicotine biosynthesis pathway. Similarly, no potential regulators or genes of the nicotine biosynthesis pathway were identified in clusters of orthologous genes that are shared between <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. undulata</i>, or <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. paniculata</i> and/or <i>N. knightiana</i>, that could have provided insights on the maternal and paternal progenitors impact on the alkaloid pathway.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Core gene set for phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>To obtain a gene set for the phylogenetic analysis, OrthoMCL was run using the groups mentioned above, as well as the ancestors of tobacco, <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, with tomato as an outgroup. A core set of 12,401 ortholog groups is shared among the species. Of these, 3041 have a group composition that corresponds to the expectations of polyploidization: one group member for the diploid species and two members for the allotetraploids. These can be aligned and used to calculate phylogenetic properties. A filtered subset of 2951 groups showed that 1250 <i>N. rustica</i> genes clustered with genes from <i>N. knightiana</i>, 1048 <i>N. rustica</i> genes clustered with genes from <i>N. paniculata</i>, and 653 <i>N. rustica</i> genes clustered with genes from the common ancestor of <i>N. knightiana</i> and <i>N. paniculata</i>. This result supports the earlier observation, based on the chloroplast genome SNP analysis, that <i>N. knightiana</i> is closer to <i>N. rustica</i> than <i>N. paniculata</i> and corroborates the phylogenic tree constructed by Sarkinen et al. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Sarkinen T, Bohs L, Olmstead RG, Knapp S. A phylogenetic framework for evolutionary study of the nightshades (Solanaceae): a dated 1000-tip tree. BMC Evol Biol. 2013;13:214." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d16228e2174">27</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Alkaloid pathways</h3><p>The alkaloid pattern was species dependent and consistent among the biological replicates of plants cultivated in greenhouse and field (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Tab2">2</a>). Under all conditions, <i>N. rustica</i> contained more nicotine in the upper and lower leaves compared with all of the other species. <i>N. paniculata</i>, <i>N. knightiana</i> and, to some extent, <i>N. undulata</i> showed high nicotine to nornicotine conversion rates in the roots, which was not the case for <i>N. rustica</i>. However, <i>N. rustica</i> contained a higher level of anatabine compared with its progenitors. In Nicotiana species, the core of the alkaloid pathway consists of 8 enzymes leading to the biosynthesis of nicotine and nornicotine. Starting from putrescine, putrescine N-methyltransferase (PMT) and N-methylputrescine oxidase (MPO) are responsible for the formation of the pyrrolidine ring of nicotine, while the pyridine ring if formed from aspartate by aspartate oxidase (AO), quinolinate synthase (QS) and quinolinate phosphoribosyl transferase (QPT). A622 and berberine bridge enzyme-like (BBL) oxidoreductases are then involved in the coupling of the two rings to form nicotine. Nicotine is further demethylated by nicotine N-demethylase (NND/CYP82E) to nornicotine. The jasmonate-inducible ERF189 and ERF199 factors are key regulators of the nicotine biosynthesis pathway in <i>N. tabacum</i>. The jasmonate signaling bHLH-family transcription factor MYC2, regulates the nicotine pathway genes by interacting with ERF189 and ERF199, and by directly binding to G box elements found in their promoters [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Kajikawa M, Sierro N, Kawaguchi H, Bakaher N, Ivanov NV, Hashimoto T, et al. Genomic insights into the evolution of the nicotine biosynthesis pathway in tobacco. Plant Physiol. 2017;174:999–1011." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d16228e2210">28</a>]. The alkaloid levels are shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Fig3">3</a> in the context of the alkaloid pathway, and the expression of the alkaloid pathway genes and key regulators at the different sampling times and in the different tissues are shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Fig4">4</a>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Akaloid concentration from plants grown in the field, young plants grown in the greenhouse, and flowering plants grown in the greenhouse</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-018-5241-5/MediaObjects/12864_2018_5241_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc"
