/></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Alkaloid synthesis pathway with the measured metabolite contents in three different organs (LL: lower leaf, UL: upper leaf, R: root) from field-grown plants. A622, an enzyme proposed to form a coupling-competent nicotinic acid intermediate; AO, L-aspartate oxidase; BBL, berberine bridge enzyme-like protein; MPO, methylputrescine oxidase; NAD, nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide; NND, nicotine N-demethylase; PMT, putrecine N-methyltransferase; QPT, quinolinate phosphoribosyltransferase; QS, quinolinate synthase</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-018-5241-5/MediaObjects/12864_2018_5241_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-018-5241-5/MediaObjects/12864_2018_5241_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1929" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Expression of genes and key regulators of the alkaloid pathway. Log10 transformed FPKM gene expression values were used for the heatmap. LL: lower leaf, UL: upper leaf, RO: root, FL: flower, GH_1: greenhouse pre-flowering, FI: field pre-flowering, and GH_2: greenhouse flowering</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>For each enzyme of the alkaloid pathway, the copy numbers of the encoding genes and their expression levels in the various organs were analyzed in <i>N. rustica</i>, <i>N. tabacum</i> and their respective ancestors to identify changes, which could explain differences in alkaloid accumulations. An additional putrescine methyltransferase (<i>PMT</i>) gene was identified in the <i>N. rustica</i> genome in comparison with the <i>N. tabacum</i> genome (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#MOESM5">5</a>: Figure S4a). From the six <i>PMT</i> genes identified in <i>N. rustica</i>, four (<i>PMT-2</i>, <i>-3</i>, <i>-5</i> and -<i>6</i>) have homologs in <i>N. undulata</i> (<i>PMT-1</i>, -<i>2</i>, -<i>3</i> and -<i>4</i>, respectively), and the two others (<i>PMT-1</i> and -<i>4</i>) likely originated from a single gene in <i>N. knightiana</i>/<i>N. paniculata</i> (<i>PMT-1</i>). Interestingly, the PMT sequences from <i>N. rustica</i> and their corresponding progenitors, except PMT-2, slightly diverged from <i>N. tabacum</i> sequences, as shown in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#MOESM5">5</a>: Figure S4a. In comparison, only four <i>PMT</i> genes were identified in <i>N. tabacum</i> as published by Riechers and Timko [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Riechers DE, Timko MP. Structure and expression of the gene family encoding putrescine N-methyltransferase in Nicotiana tabacum: new clues to the evolutionary origin of cultivated tobacco. Plant Mol Biol. 1999;41:387–401." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9059">29</a>] and deposited in the Uniprot database. Three (<i>PMT-2</i>, -<i>3</i> and -<i>4</i>) have homologs in <i>N. sylvestris</i> (<i>PMT-1</i>, -<i>2</i> and -<i>3</i>), and one (<i>PMT-1</i>) in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> (<i>PMT-1</i>, based on intron and promoter sequence comparisons; data not shown). In <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, a second copy close to <i>PMT-1</i> is present (<i>PMT-2</i>). In the <i>N. tabacum</i> genome, an additional small <i>PMT</i> fragment was also found, likely originating from the <i>N. tomentosiformis PMT-1</i> copy.</p><p>At the transcriptional level, the <i>PMT</i> genes were expressed almost exclusively in roots [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Riechers DE, Timko MP. Structure and expression of the gene family encoding putrescine N-methyltransferase in Nicotiana tabacum: new clues to the evolutionary origin of cultivated tobacco. Plant Mol Biol. 1999;41:387–401." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9119">29</a>]. In <i>N. rustica</i>, only five <i>PMT</i> genes (<i>PMT-1</i>, -<i>3</i>, -<i>4</i>, -<i>5</i> and -<i>6</i>) were expressed under our experimental conditions, while <i>PMT-2</i> and its homolog in <i>N. undulata</i> (<i>PMT-2</i>) were silent.</p><p>In <i>N. tabacum</i>, the four <i>PMT</i> genes (<i>PMT-1</i>, -<i>2</i>, -<i>3</i> and -<i>4</i>) were expressed. In <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, the second copy (<i>PMT-2</i>) was not expressed under our experimental conditions. This lack of expression could result from the two-fold reduction of putative binding sites for transcription factors in the promoter of <i>PMT-2</i> (based on JASPAR predictions) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Xu B, Timko M. Methyl jasmonate induced expression of the tobacco putrescine N-methyltransferase genes requires both G-box and GCC-motif elements. Plant Mol Biol. 2004;55:743–61." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9185">30</a>]. Because <i>PMT</i> is a key gene in nicotine metabolism [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Teuber M, Azemi ME, Namjoyan F, Meier AC, Wodak A, Brandt W, et al. Putrescine N-methyltransferases--a structure-function analysis. Plant Mol Biol. 2007;63:787–801." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9192">31</a>], the addition of one more expressed gene in <i>N. rustica</i> compared with <i>N. tabacum</i> may increase nicotine biosynthesis in <i>N. rustica</i>. Based on the data presented in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#MOESM5">5</a>: Figure S4a, we hypothesize that a main difference between <i>N. rustica</i> and its progenitors is the root-to-shoot transport of nicotine.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Nornicotine biosynthesis</h3><p>Nornicotine is generated through the N-demethylation of nicotine, which involves cytochrome P450 nicotine N-demethylases (NNDs). In tobacco leaves, three active enzymes, CYP82E4, CYP82E5 and CYP82E10, have been identified [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Lewis RS, Bowen SW, Keogh MR, Dewey RE. Three nicotine demethylase genes mediate nornicotine biosynthesis in Nicotiana tabacum L.: functional characterization of the CYP82E10 gene. Phytochemistry. 2010;71:1988–98." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9218">32</a>]. As shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Fig3">3</a>, the nornicotine content may vary between <i>N. rustica</i> and its progenitors, as well as between <i>N. tabacum</i> and its progenitors. The nornicotine content is particularly abundant in the roots of <i>N. rustica</i> progenitors as compared with that in the lower leaves of <i>N. rustica</i> (five to nine times more elevated) and with <i>N. sylvestris</i> (two times more abundant compared with upper leaves and roots) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Fig3">3</a>). To determine whether a correlation exists between gene expression and the nornicotine content, a phylogenetic tree based on sequences and sequence fragments identified in the genomes was generated. A large number of translated sequences encoding <i>CYP82E</i> genes, nine in <i>N. rustica</i>, eight in <i>N. paniculata</i>, six in <i>N. knightiana</i>, seven in <i>N. undulata</i>, 17 in <i>N. tabacum</i>, 10 in <i>N. sylvestris</i> and seven in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, were found. Nornicotine is approximately five, eight and nine times more elevated in the roots of <i>N. undulata</i>, <i>N. paniculata</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i>, respectively, than in the roots of <i>N. rustica</i>. This is in agreement with the reduced expression levels of the two <i>N. rustica NND</i> genes, <i>NND2</i> (10.25 fragments/kb/million mapped reads [FPKM]) and <i>NND7</i> (10.53 FPKM), compared with their corresponding progenitor genes in <i>N. paniculata</i>, <i>NND1</i> (166.88 FPKM) and <i>NND4</i> (127.4 FPKM), <i>N. knightiana</i>, <i>NND1</i> (145.69 FPKM), and <i>N. undulata</i>, <i>NND7</i> (33.01 FPKM). Furthermore, <i>N. undulata</i> and <i>N. paniculata</i> also have one more expressed gene each, <i>NND1</i> (33.88 FPKM) and <i>NND2</i> (96.85 FPKM), respectively, which are not present in <i>N. rustica</i>. The observations made at the transcriptional level were valid under field and greenhouse conditions. Thus, both the reduced expression levels of the <i>N. rustica NND</i> accessions compared with the corresponding progenitor accessions, and the presence of additional transcripts in <i>N. rustica</i> progenitors may contribute to the elevated nornicotine concentrations in the roots.</p><p>In <i>N. sylvestris</i>, nornicotine is approximately two and five times more abundant than in <i>N. tabacum</i> in the lower and upper leaves, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Fig3">3</a>). This observation was correlated with the higher transcript levels of <i>N. sylvestris NND7</i> (<i>CYP82E2</i>), <i>NND1</i> (<i>CYP82E10</i>) and <i>NND9</i> compared with the corresponding genes in <i>N. tabacum</i>, <i>NND9</i> (<i>CYP82E2</i>), <i>NND3</i> (<i>CYP82E10</i>) and <i>NND17</i>, respectively (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#MOESM5">5</a>: Figure S4b). We have to consider as well that mutations play a role for the activity of NNDs, as demonstrated for CYP82E2 and CYP82E3 not being active in <i>N. tabacum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Lewis RS, Bowen SW, Keogh MR, Dewey RE. Three nicotine demethylase genes mediate nornicotine biosynthesis in Nicotiana tabacum L.: functional characterization of the CYP82E10 gene. Phytochemistry. 2010;71:1988–98." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9388">32</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Leaf nicotine accumulation</h3><p>The transport of both anatabine and nicotine from root-to-shoot increased in both <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. tabacum</i> compared with their progenitors, particularly for mature plants grown in the greenhouse and field (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Tab2">2</a>). This suggests that the improved transport of both alkaloids may result from the combination of the progenitor genome after the allotetraploid formation. Because all alkaloid transporters are not yet identified in plants, particularly the transporter(s) controlling nicotine translocation from root-to-shoot [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Shitan N, Hayashida M, Yazaki K. Translocation and accumulation of nicotine via distinct spatio-temporal regulation of nicotine transporters in Nicotiana tabacum. Plant Signal Behav. 2015;10:e1035852." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9408">33</a>], we looked at genes being co-expressed with anatabine and nicotine profiles. For this purpose, we calculated the correlations between the expression levels of every gene with the alkaloid concentrations across all tissues, and selected the most highly correlated for a more detailed inspection. For anatabine, no transcripts with high correlations were found; however, the transcripts of two ABC transporters, named MRP2A and MRP2B owing to their high homology with Arabidopsis multidrug resistance-associated protein 2 (MRP2, AtABCC2), exhibited high correlations (0.96 and 0.93, respectively) with nicotine in <i>N. rustica</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Fig5">5</a>). <i>MRP2A</i> is from the <i>N. undulata</i> progenitor, and <i>MRP2B</i> is from the <i>N. paniculata</i> or <i>N. knightiana</i> progenitor. In Arabidopsis, AtABCC2 (MRP2) has two different activities: (1) in association with AtABCC1, AtABCC2 confer tolerance to cadmium and mercury, in addition to their role in arsenic detoxification, possibly involving phytochelatin detoxification process [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Park J, Song W-Y, Ko D, Eom Y, Hansen TH, Schiller M, et al. The phytochelatin transporters AtABCC1 and AtABCC2 mediate tolerance to cadmium and mercury: ABC transporters for PC-dependent cd and hg tolerance. Plant J. 2012;69:278–88." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9434">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Song W-Y, Park J, Mendoza-Cozatl DG, Suter-Grotemeyer M, Shim D, Hortensteiner S, et al. Arsenic tolerance in Arabidopsis is mediated by two ABCC-type phytochelatin transporters. Proc Natl Acad Sci. 2010;107:21187–92." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9434_1">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Song W-Y, Mendoza-CóZatl DG, Lee Y, Schroeder JI, Ahn S-N, Lee H-S, et al. Phytochelatin-metal(loid) transport into vacuoles shows different substrate preferences in barley and Arabidopsis: Phytochelatin-metal(loid) vacuolar transport in barley. Plant Cell Environ. 2014;37:1192–201." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9434_2">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Frelet-Barrand A, Kolukisaoglu HÜ, Plaza S, Rüffer M, Azevedo L, Hörtensteiner S, et al. Comparative mutant analysis of Arabidopsis ABCC-type ABC transporters: AtMRP2 contributes to detoxification, vacuolar organic anion transport and chlorophyll degradation. Plant Cell Physiol. 2008;49:557–69." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9437">37</a>] (2) this ABC transporter is also involved in vacuolar transport of chlorophyll catabolites [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Frelet-Barrand A, Kolukisaoglu HÜ, Plaza S, Rüffer M, Azevedo L, Hörtensteiner S, et al. Comparative mutant analysis of Arabidopsis ABCC-type ABC transporters: AtMRP2 contributes to detoxification, vacuolar organic anion transport and chlorophyll degradation. Plant Cell Physiol. 2008;49:557–69." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9440">37</a>].</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-018-5241-5/MediaObjects/12864_2018_5241_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-018-5241-5/MediaObjects/12864_2018_5241_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1229" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Profile alignments of the nicotine content and <i>MRP2A</i> and <i>MRP2B</i> transcripts in <i>N. rustica</i> (Nrus) (<b>a</b>) and <i>N. tabacum</i> TN90 (<b>b</b>) in the roots (Root YP), and lower (LL YP) and upper (UL YP) leaves of young plants grown in the greenhouse, in the roots (Root Field), lower (LL Field) and upper (UL Field) leaves of plants grown in the field, and finally in the roots (Root FP), flower (Flower FP) and lower (LL FP) and upper (UL FP) leaves of flowering plants grown in the greenhouse. Profiles of asparagine (Asn), glutamine (Gln), aspartate (Asp) and glutamate (Glu) contents (mg/g DW) in <i>N. rustica</i> (Nrus) and its progenitors, <i>N. paniculata</i> (Npan), <i>N. knightiana</i> (Nkni) and <i>N. undulata</i> (Nund) (<b>c</b>) and <i>N. tabacum</i> (Ntab) and its progenitors, <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> (Ntom) and <i>N. sylvestris</i> (Nsyl) (<b>d</b>). Data were collected from flowering plants in the greenhouse. Sodium (Na) and potassium (K) profiles in <i>N. rustica</i> and its progenitors (<b>e</b> and <b>f</b>) as well as in <i>N. tabacum</i> and its progenitors (<b>g</b> and <b>h</b>). Data were collected in young plants grown in the greenhouse, but root data are missing for <i>N. undulata</i>. LL: lower leaf, UL: upper leaf, R: root, FL: flower, GH_1: greenhouse pre-flowering, FI_1: field pre-flowering and GH_2: greenhouse flowering. In graphic <b>a</b>, <b>b</b>, <b>f</b> and <b>h</b>, curves were aligned to ease comparison independently of the calculated correlations</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In contrast to <i>N. rustica</i>, <i>MRP2A</i> and <i>MRP2B</i> transcript profiles were not correlated with nicotine in <i>N. tabacum</i> TN90. <i>MRP2A</i> was inherited from the <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> progenitor and <i>MRP2B</i> from the <i>N. sylvestris</i> progenitor. These data suggest that the activities of <i>N. rustica MRP2</i> genes may be triggered by the nicotine content or play a role in the nicotine root-to-shoot transport of <i>N. rustica</i> specifically. In both progenitors of the allotetraploids <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. tabacum</i>, <i>MRP2</i> transcript profiles exhibited no correlation with the nicotine profiles (data not shown), suggesting that the synchronization of <i>MRP2A</i> and <i>MRP2B</i> transcripts with nicotine occurred after the hybridization of parental genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Amino acid pathways involving glutamate, glutamine (Gln), aspartate and asparagine</h3><p>Glutamate, Gln, aspartate and asparagine are central regulators of nitrogen assimilation, metabolism and transport. In both <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. tabacum</i> progenitors, Gln is abundant in the flowers of greenhouse-cultivated plants. Similarly high levels of Gln are also found in <i>N. tabacum</i> but not in <i>N. rustica</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Fig5">5</a>). Gln synthesis is dependent on the glutamate synthetase (GS) activity, which plays an essential role in nitrogen metabolism. The enzyme catalyzes the condensation of glutamate and ammonia to form Gln. We identified 9 and 10 different <i>GS</i> genes in <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. tabacum</i>, respectively, all of which were expressed. A phylogenetic tree grouped the <i>GS</i> genes into four clusters (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#MOESM5">5</a>: Figure S4c), independent of the Nicotiana species, thereby indicating the importance of sequence conservation during evolution. The expression data aligned with the gene clustering, highlighting the correlation between structure and function in this gene family.</p><p>Additionally, expression data helped to identify the candidate genes responsible for the regulation of Gln formation in flowers (Cluster I). Indeed, <i>N. knightiana GS-1</i> and its homolog <i>N. paniculata GS-4</i> are more than eight times more highly expressed in flowers than the corresponding genes of <i>N. rustica</i> (<i>GS-3</i>), whereas <i>N. undulata GS-5</i> is more than four times more highly expressed than the corresponding gene of <i>N. rustica</i> (<i>GS-7</i>). However, the reason for the lower expression levels of <i>N. rustica GS-3</i> and <i>GS-7</i> than the corresponding progenitor genes remains obscure. The orthologous genes of <i>N. tabacum</i> (<i>GS-8</i> and <i>GS-10</i>, respectively) belonging to the same cluster are less downregulated (&lt; 2.5×) than the corresponding genes in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> (<i>GS-3</i>) and <i>N. sylvestris</i> (<i>GS-4</i>). In addition, <i>N. sylvestris GS-4</i> has an additional copy in <i>N. tabacum (GS-6</i>) which may contribute to there being more Gln in <i>N. tabacum</i> than in <i>N. rustica</i> flowers.</p><p>All of these clustered genes, which are particularly expressed in non-photosynthetic tissues, like flowers and mature roots, are cytosolic <i>GS1</i>s that are involved specifically in the remobilization of nitrogen for seed feeding [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Bernard SM, Habash DZ. The importance of cytosolic glutamine synthetase in nitrogen assimilation and recycling. New Phytol. 2009;182:608–20." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9705">38</a>].</p><p>The observations made above for GS are not applicable for asparagine synthetase (<i>ASN</i>, data not shown), although the amino acid data suggested strong ASN activities in roots and flowers of <i>N. sylvestris</i> and in flowers of <i>N. tabacum</i> compared with other tissues, and with <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. rustica</i> progenitors. This may be because asparagine synthesis depends on multiple factors, like the size of the Gln and aspartate pools, as well as the level of ATP as an energy source [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Arnold A, Sajitz-Hermstein M, Nikoloski Z. Effects of varying nitrogen sources on amino acid synthesis costs in Arabidopsis thaliana under different light and carbon-source conditions. PLoS One. 2015;10:e0116536." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9727">39</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Selinski J, Scheibe R. Lack of malate valve capacities lead to improved N-assimilation and growth in transgenic a. thaliana plants. Plant Signal Behav. 2014;9:e29057." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9730">40</a>], and not just gene activation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Metal accumulations</h3><p><i>N. rustica</i> accumulates less cadmium (Cd) in leaves than <i>N. tabacum</i> under hydroponic conditions and at relatively high Cd concentrations (1 μM) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bovet L, Rossi L, Lugon-Moulin N. Cadmium partitioning and gene expression studies in Nicotiana tabacum and Nicotiana rustica. Physiol Plant. 2006;128:466–75." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9746">6</a>]. In the data presented in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S2, under field and greenhouse conditions, no major differences were found, confirming previously published data [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bovet L, Rossi L, Lugon-Moulin N. Cadmium partitioning and gene expression studies in Nicotiana tabacum and Nicotiana rustica. Physiol Plant. 2006;128:466–75." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9752">6</a>], suggesting that leaf Cd accumulation is different when <i>N.rustica</i> and <i>N.tabacum</i> are grown under non- or Cd contaminated soils. Therefore it is not surprising to find no significant correlation between nicotine and cadmium in our dataset, soil used in the depicted experiments being low in Cd. However, this doesn’t exclude that Cd may interfere with nicotine synthesis or transport in high Cd contaminated soils or artificial nutrient solutions, therefore possibly involving homologous <i>AtABCC2</i> genes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Park J, Song W-Y, Ko D, Eom Y, Hansen TH, Schiller M, et al. The phytochelatin transporters AtABCC1 and AtABCC2 mediate tolerance to cadmium and mercury: ABC transporters for PC-dependent cd and hg tolerance. Plant J. 2012;69:278–88." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9765">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Song W-Y, Park J, Mendoza-Cozatl DG, Suter-Grotemeyer M, Shim D, Hortensteiner S, et al. Arsenic tolerance in Arabidopsis is mediated by two ABCC-type phytochelatin transporters. Proc Natl Acad Sci. 2010;107:21187–92." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9765_1">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Song W-Y, Mendoza-CóZatl DG, Lee Y, Schroeder JI, Ahn S-N, Lee H-S, et al. Phytochelatin-metal(loid) transport into vacuoles shows different substrate preferences in barley and Arabidopsis: Phytochelatin-metal(loid) vacuolar transport in barley. Plant Cell Environ. 2014;37:1192–201." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9765_2">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Frelet-Barrand A, Kolukisaoglu HÜ, Plaza S, Rüffer M, Azevedo L, Hörtensteiner S, et al. Comparative mutant analysis of Arabidopsis ABCC-type ABC transporters: AtMRP2 contributes to detoxification, vacuolar organic anion transport and chlorophyll degradation. Plant Cell Physiol. 2008;49:557–69." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9768">37</a>]. We also determined the concentrations of other metals and ions. For instance, <i>N. rustica</i> accumulated more arsenic in the root compared to <i>N. tabacum</i>, particularly under greenhouse condition. This observation is possibly correlated with the accumulation of sulfur in <i>N. rustica</i> and its ancestor <i>N. knightiana</i>, sulfur being known to play a role in the arsenic detoxification via the production of glutathione and glutathione-S-transferase [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Kumar S, Trivedi PK. Glutathione S-transferases: role in combating abiotic stresses including arsenic detoxification in plants. Front Plant Sci. 2018;9. &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2018.00751&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9784">41</a>] or even phytochelatins. In this respect, ABCC2 homologs are also possibly involved in some detoxification process [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Song W-Y, Park J, Mendoza-Cozatl DG, Suter-Grotemeyer M, Shim D, Hortensteiner S, et al. Arsenic tolerance in Arabidopsis is mediated by two ABCC-type phytochelatin transporters. Proc Natl Acad Sci. 2010;107:21187–92." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9787">35</a>]. The data on potassium (K) and sodium (Na) levels showed that, independent of the environmental growth conditions, the accumulation profiles in both roots and leaves were similar under greenhouse and field conditions in <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. rustica</i> progenitors, and much higher than in <i>N. tabacum</i> and its progenitors. This suggests that in <i>N. rustica</i> the maintenance of K and Na homeostases is robust and crucial for growth. However, this observation is less valid for <i>N. tabacum</i> and its progenitors, which showed more variation in K in greenhouse flowering leaves (GH_2, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Fig5">5</a>), and for Na in <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> in field leaves (Ntom-UL and Ntom-LL, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#Fig5">5</a>), suggesting that K and Na may be subjected to more plasticity in <i>N. tabacum</i> and its progenitors.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><p>Following the publication of the genome of <i>N. tabacum</i> and its progenitors, a second, independent, set of genomes containing a tetraploid Nicotiana species, <i>N. rustica,</i> and its progenitors is available to investigate the impact of speciation by hybridization of two diploid species. Unlike in <i>N. tabacum</i>, no intergenomic translocations were observed in <i>N. rustica</i>. Those present in <i>N. tabacum</i> are hypothesized to be the result of the wider divergence between the parental genomes of <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> compared with those of <i>N. rustica</i>, which has relatively close parental genomes from <i>N. undulata</i> and Nicotiana section Paniculata. It is possible that the similar parental genomes of <i>N. rustica</i> exert a lower “genomic stress” [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Lim KY, Matyasek R, Kovarik A, Leitch AR. Genome evolution in allotetraploid Nicotiana. Biol J Linn Soc. 2004;82:599–606." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9859">18</a>], resulting in less selection pressure and, therefore, less intergenomic translocations. Despite not giving a final answer regarding the sequence of evolutionary events leading to the speciation of <i>N. rustica</i>, <i>N. paniculata</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i>, the genome sequences of the progenitors of <i>N. rustica</i> also indicate that <i>N. knightiana</i> is more closely related to <i>N. rustica</i> than <i>N. paniculata</i>.</p><p>Focusing on the mechanism behind the upregulation of nicotine production in <i>N. rustica</i> provided insights into the metabolic and genomic differences in comparison with <i>N. tabacum</i> and its progenitors. Compared with its progenitors and with <i>N. tabacum</i> and its progenitors, <i>N. rustica</i> contained more nicotine in the upper and lower leaves. Our data suggests that nicotine level in <i>N. rustica</i> results more of a genome combination of the ancestors than in <i>N. tabacum</i>, <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> exhibiting rather low nicotine in both root and leaves compared to <i>N. sylvestris</i>. Interestingly, <i>N. sylvestris</i> has three active copies of <i>PMT</i>, one of the key root expressed gene involved in nicotine synthesis, whereas <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> has only one. Therefore, four active <i>PMTs</i> were identified in <i>N.tabacum</i>, and five in <i>N. rustica</i>, for whom the genetic origin of the ancestors is more complex to draw. In addition to more active synthesis, the elevated nicotine content in <i>N. rustica</i> compared to <i>N. tabacum</i> may also results from a more active transport to the shoot via an ABC transporter, its expression being correlated with nicotine in <i>N. rustica</i>. The nicotine to nornicotine conversion rates in the roots of <i>N. paniculata</i>, <i>N. knightiana</i> and, to some extent, <i>N. undulata</i> was high, which was not the case for <i>N. rustica</i>, which contained higher levels of anatabine compared with its progenitors. Regarding the nicotine conversion, it does not seem to derive from a simple additive gene effect, nornicotine levels being generally higher in the ancestors compared to both <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. tabacum</i>. This suggests some regulatory processes to occur at the <i>CYP82E</i> transcript level. Based on our data, no interconnection between Cd uptake and nicotine synthesis as well as shoot translocation can be established. Similar experiments should be performed using Cd contaminated soils. In this context, <i>ABBC2</i> (<i>MRP2</i>) homologous genes may play a role in interfering between nicotine and Cd accumulation, AtABCC2 carrying already different substrate affinity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Park J, Song W-Y, Ko D, Eom Y, Hansen TH, Schiller M, et al. The phytochelatin transporters AtABCC1 and AtABCC2 mediate tolerance to cadmium and mercury: ABC transporters for PC-dependent cd and hg tolerance. Plant J. 2012;69:278–88." href="#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9970">34</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Song W-Y, Park J, Mendoza-Cozatl DG, Suter-Grotemeyer M, Shim D, Hortensteiner S, et al. Arsenic tolerance in Arabidopsis is mediated by two ABCC-type phytochelatin transporters. Proc Natl Acad Sci. 2010;107:21187–92." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9970_1">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Song W-Y, Mendoza-CóZatl DG, Lee Y, Schroeder JI, Ahn S-N, Lee H-S, et al. Phytochelatin-metal(loid) transport into vacuoles shows different substrate preferences in barley and Arabidopsis: Phytochelatin-metal(loid) vacuolar transport in barley. Plant Cell Environ. 2014;37:1192–201." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9970_2">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Frelet-Barrand A, Kolukisaoglu HÜ, Plaza S, Rüffer M, Azevedo L, Hörtensteiner S, et al. Comparative mutant analysis of Arabidopsis ABCC-type ABC transporters: AtMRP2 contributes to detoxification, vacuolar organic anion transport and chlorophyll degradation. Plant Cell Physiol. 2008;49:557–69." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d16228e9973">37</a>]. About two times more asparagine and glutamine were found in the flower of <i>N. tabacum</i> compared to <i>N. rustica</i> suggesting a more efficient remobilization of carbon and nitrogen resources, possibly supported by different glutamine synthase activities. Finally<i>, N. rustica</i> accumulates more sulphur than <i>N. tabacum</i> particularly in the above ground organs which may support arsenic and cadmium detoxification under certain growth conditions. In addition, K and Na homeostases seem to be particularly well-controlled in <i>N. rustica</i> compared to <i>N. tabacum</i> for the maintenance of growth.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec16" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec16-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec16">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec16-content"><p>The comparative genome analysis of four related Nicotiana genomes showed that the tetraploid species <i>N. rustica</i> inherited about 41% of its genome from its paternal progenitor, <i>N. undulata</i>, the rest originating for its maternal progenitor, the common ancestor of <i>N. paniculata</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i>. Analysis of the genome sequences of the progenitors of <i>N. rustica</i> indicated that <i>N. knightiana</i> is more closely related to <i>N. rustica</i> than <i>N. paniculata</i>, although the sequence of evolutionary events leading to the speciation of <i>N. rustica</i> remain to be elucidated. <i>N. rustica</i> contained more nicotine in the upper and lower leaves than its progenitors, this nicotine level likely being the results of the genome combination of the progenitors. A more active transport of nicotine to the shoot via an ABC transporter in <i>N. rustica</i> may also contribute to the elevated nicotine content in <i>N. rustica</i> compared to <i>N. tabacum</i> in addition to the presence of one more additional <i>PMT</i> copy in the <i>N. rustica</i> genome. The availability of these new set of related Nicotiana genome sequences, will significantly contribute to better understanding the impact of speciation and the evolution of tetraploid Nicotiana species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec17" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec17-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec17">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec17-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Plant material</h3><p><i>N. rustica</i> L. var. Brasilia No. 7 (PI 499174, TR13, USDA–GRIN database), <i>N. paniculata</i> L. (PI 555545, TW 99, USDA–GRIN database), <i>N. knightiana</i> Goodsp. (PI 555527, TW 73, USDA–GRIN database), <i>N. undulata</i> Ruiz &amp; Pav. (PI 555575, TW 146, USDA–GRIN database), <i>N. tabacum</i> L. cv. TN90 (PI 543792, TC 586, USDA–GRIN database), <i>N. sylvestris</i> Speg. &amp; Comes (PI 555569, TW 136, USDA–GRIN database) and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> Goodsp. (PI 555572, TW 142, USDA–GRIN database) were used in the experiments.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Plantlet growth and hydroponics</h3><p>Seeds were sown on soil. For the field experiment, plants were transferred after 3 weeks into soil-containing floating trays (floating tray solution: Hauert Plantaaktiv 15 + 7 + 22; Hauert, Grossaffoltern, Switzerland). Plants were grown for six more weeks in hydroponics before being transferred to the field. For the greenhouse experiment, plants were directly transferred to pots.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Greenhouse experiment</h3><p>The solutions used for the fertilization of soil-grown plants were purchased from Yara Benelux B.V. (Vlaardingen, The Netherlands) and contained (per liter): 605.62 mg NO<sub>3</sub>, 13.29 mg NH<sub>4</sub> (total of 147.77 mg N), 65.56 mg P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, 275.44 mg K<sub>2</sub>O, 35.89 mg Mg, 133.26 mg Ca, 265.35 mg SO<sub>4</sub>, 0.516 mg Fe, 0.338 mg Mn, 0.201 mg Zn, 0.199 mg B, 0.029 mg Cu and 0.03 mg Mo. All of the plants were grown in a 16-h light:8-h dark cycle. First time points for plant sampling took place after 10 weeks of growth. At this time point, <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. paniculata</i> were already flowering, and <i>N. knightiana</i> and <i>N. sylvestris</i> were starting to flower. Next, plants were sampled again at a second time point when fully flowering. <i>N. rustica</i>, <i>N. paniculata</i>, <i>N. knightiana</i> and <i>N. sylvestris</i> were sampled after 12 weeks of growth, <i>N. tabacum</i> and <i>N. undulata</i> after 14 weeks and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> was transferred to a 9-h light:15-h dark cycle to induce flowering and then sampled after 21 weeks.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Field trial</h3><p>The field experiment was conducted in Switzerland (Vaud). Prior to the field experiment, soil samples were taken to a depth of 30 cm, and mixed and analyzed by Sol Conseil (Changins, Switzerland). Furthermore, shortly before transplanting, an additional soil sample (30-cm depth) was analyzed for nitrogen content (Service de l’agriculture, Agrilogie, Grange-Verney, Moudon, Switzerland). The soil was composed of 14.1% clay, 35.5% silt and 50.4% sand, containing 1.8% organic matter, with a pH of 8.0. The soil contained 140 mg kg<sup>− 1</sup> soluble Ca and, as determined by ammonium acetate-EDTA extraction, 27.0 mg kg<sup>− 1</sup> P, 157.6 mg kg<sup>− 1</sup> K, 50,234 mg kg<sup>− 1</sup> Ca and 361.7 mg kg<sup>− 1</sup> Mg. The total N was 25.9 kg ha<sup>− 1</sup>, composed of 23.2 kg ha<sup>− 1</sup> N-NO<sub>3</sub> and 2.7 kg ha<sup>− 1</sup> N-NH<sub>4</sub>. The field was fertilized according to tobacco cultivation practices with K<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> [450 kg ha<sup>− 1</sup> 50% + S; Landor (Birsfelden, Switzerland)], superphosphate [239 kg ha<sup>− 1</sup> 18 P-4 Mg; Landor (Birsfelden, Switzerland)] and nitrochalk [97 kg ha<sup>− 1</sup> total 15.5–0-0; Yara Benelux B.V. (Vlaardingen, The Netherlands)]. During the season, precipitation was measured using a rain gauge. Temperature was obtained from the nearest weather station (MétéoSuisse). The growing season was relatively dry and hot. The temperature exceeded 30 °C on 23 days (maximum of 37.9 °C) and 144 mm of rainfall were recorded. The field was irrigated twice with a total of 45 mm of water. Plants were grown in a random design. Four plants per species were harvested 80 days after transplantation. Plants were at a different physiological stages, depending on the plant species. While <i>N. rustica</i>, <i>N. paniculata</i>, <i>N. knightiana</i> and <i>N. sylvestris</i> were flowering, <i>N. tabacum</i>, <i>N. undulata</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> were not yet flowering. <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> grew very poorly in the field. Total plants, including roots, were harvested and processed as described below (section “Plant sampling”).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Plant sampling</h3><p>Roots were separated from shoots and washed with water until clean. Roots were dried with paper and flash-frozen in liquid nitrogen. They were ground in liquid nitrogen and some of the material was used for RNA extraction. Some ground root material was lyophilized and then analyzed for alkaloid, amino acid and elemental compositions. One or more leaves positioned on the lower stalks (depending on leaf size) were harvested for each plant and leaves were cut in halves (without the midrib). One half was frozen in liquid nitrogen for RNA analysis and the other half-leaf was lyophilized and analyzed for alkaloid and elemental compositions. One or more leaves positioned on the upper stalks were similarly processed. The leaf materials for RNA extractions were ground in liquid nitrogen.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Data analysis</h3><p>Four plants were analyzed for each variety and condition. Values are means ± standard deviations of four replicate plants. When the value was below the limit of reporting, the reporting limit was used for the calculation. Owing to insufficient materials being available for collection at the first sampling time point in the greenhouse, only two <i>N. paniculata</i> root samples, three <i>N. sylvestris</i> root samples and no <i>N. undulata</i> root samples were analyzed for elemental composition. However, all of the samples were analyzed for alkaloids and amino acids.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Elemental analysis</h3><p>The elemental composition of the samples was analyzed by ALS Life Sciences (Praha, Czech Republic). Samples were homogenized and mineralized by acids and hydrogen peroxide prior to analysis (CZ_SOP_D06_02_J02 chap. 10.17.1, 10.17.2, 10.17.4, 10.17.7, 10.17.8). As, Cd, Cr, Cu, Pb, Ni and Zn were measured by mass spectrometry with inductively coupled plasma according to CZ_SOP_D06_02_002 (US EPA 200.8, CSN EN ISO 17294-2). The elemental composition always refers to plant dry weight. All other elements were measured by inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectroscopy according to CZ_SOP_D06_02_001 (US EPA 200.7, ISO 11885).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">LC-UV/MS analysis of freeze-dried plant material</h3><p>Weighed aliquots (~ 25 mg) of lyophilized and pulverized plant materials were extracted with 1.8 mL of 0.1 N HCl at 90 °C for 1 h. After centrifugation, 120 μL aliquots of the supernatants were mixed with 800 μL MeCN, 40 μL of 0.33 M sodium acetate solution; and centrifuged again in a solution of isotopically labelled internal standards (215 μg/mL K<sup>15</sup>NO<sub>3</sub>; 2.0 μg/mL nornicotine-<i>d</i><sub>4</sub>; 40 μg/mL nicotine-<i>d</i><sub>4</sub>; 1.0 μg/mL anatabine-<i>d</i><sub>4</sub>; and 10 μg/mL asparagine-<sup>15</sup><i>N</i><sub>2</sub>; in MeOH). The supernatants were analyzed by LC-MS on an Ultimate 3000 UHPLC system coupled to a Q-Exactive mass spectrometer (Thermo Fisher Scientific). Chromatographic separation was performed on an Acquity UPLC BEH Amide column (1.7 μm, 150 × 2.1 mm; Waters), and the column temperature was set to 20 °C. Eluents were aqueous ammonium formate (2 mM) with added formic acid (0.25% <i>v</i>/v; eluent A) and MeCN with added formic acid (0.1% v/v; eluent B) applied as a gradient (0 min–6% A; 0.5 min–6% A; 4.0 min–60% A; 4.5 min–60% A; flow: 0.5 mL/min). The injection volume was 0.7 μL. Nitrate, nicotine, anatabine, nornicotine, glutamic acid, Gln, aspartic acid and asparagine were eluted after 1.15, 3.16, 3.23, 3.32, 4.00, 4.10, 4.16 and 4.20 min, respectively. For MS detection, electrospray ionization was applied with capillary voltages of 3.7 and 2.0 kV in positive and negative modes, respectively. The nitrate ion was detected in the negative mode, while nicotine and the amino acids were detected as [M + H]<sup>+</sup> pseudomolecular ions in the positive mode. For the detection of anatabine, the <i>m/z</i> 25 fragment was used after collision-induced fragmentation of the <i>m/z</i> 158 ion in the positive mode. For quantification, the respective isotopically labeled internal standards were used for nitrate, nicotine, anatabine and asparagine. Aspartic acid, Gln and glutamic acid were quantified by external calibration.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Genome sequencing</h3><p>DNA extractions were performed on the aerial parts of one plant per variety using the Qiagen DNAeasy Plant Maxi Kit (Qiagen, Hilden, Germany). Short insert “paired-end” libraries were prepared using the Illumina TruSeq DNA Sample Preparation Kit version 2 (Illumina, San Diego, CA). Long insert “mate-pair” libraries were prepared according to the Nextera Mate Pair Library Prep Kit (Illumina, San Diego, CA). All of the libraries (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S3) were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq-2500 using version 3 chemistry and flow-cells with runs of 2 × 100 bases. Base calling and sample demultiplexing were performed using Illumina HiSeq Control Software and the CASAVA pipeline software. For <i>N. rustica</i>, 5- and 10-kb-long read libraries were prepared and sequenced on a Pacific Biosciences RSII.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Genome size estimation</h3><p>The genome sizes were estimated using the 31-k-mer depth distribution of all paired-end sequencing libraries, as described previously. Briefly, the genome sizes were obtained by dividing the total number of 31-k-mers considered to be error-free by their most frequent depths of coverage.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">De novo genome assembly</h3><p>Raw paired-end DNA reads were preprocessed with Trimmomatic (<a href="http://www.usadellab.org/cms/?page=trimmomatic">http://www.usadellab.org/cms/?page=trimmomatic</a>) to remove sequencing adapters and low quality reads from the 5′ and 3′ ends of the reads, and to discard reads shorter than 50 bp. Raw mate-paired DNA reads were preprocessed with NxTrim (<a href="https://github.com/sequencing/NxTrim">https://github.com/sequencing/NxTrim</a>) to separate them into mate-pairs and paired-ends based on the presence of the Nextera adapter. The clean reads were then assembled into contigs using SOAPdenovo2 (<a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/soapdenovo.html">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/soapdenovo.html</a>) with a k-mer of 63 and scaffolded by increasing library size. Gaps resulting from the scaffolding were closed using GapCloser (<a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/soapdenovo.html">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/soapdenovo.html</a>), and all sequences shorter than 200 bases were discarded from the final assemblies. After closing the gaps, singletons were used as queries in a BLAST-based algorithm against the scaffolds. They were eliminated if the match level was greater than 97% to avoid artificial duplications of short sequences. Long Pacific Biosciences reads were used to further scaffold the <i>N. rustica</i> assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Repeat content estimation</h3><p>The repeat contents of the genome assemblies were estimated using RepeatMasker (<a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org">http://www.repeatmasker.org</a>) with the eudicot repeat library available from the Sol Genomics Network, the TIGR Solanaceae repeat library and a RepeatScout (<a href="https://bix.ucsd.edu/repeatscout/">https://bix.ucsd.edu/repeatscout</a>) library created using sequences of at least 150 kb from the draft genome assembly. The classification of the repeat types was performed using hits to known repeat elements achieved by a BLASTN algorithm-based search.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec30">Assessment of genome completeness</h3><p>The completeness of the genomes was assessed using Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO, <a href="http://busco.ezlab.org">http://busco.ezlab.org</a>) with the embryophyta plant dataset consisting of 1440 universal single-copy orthologs. In addition, the numbers of unique ITAG 2.3 and TAIR 10 proteins mapping to the Nicotiana genomes were determined using BLAT (<a href="http://www.kentinformatics.com">http://www.kentinformatics.com</a>) with cutoffs of 80% coverage and 80% identity for ITAG 2.3 or 60% identify for TAIR 10 (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#MOESM2">2</a>: Table S4).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec31">Transcriptome sequencing and assembly</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec32">Samples</h4><p>For <i>N. rustica</i>, two transcriptomes were generated. The first transcriptome used RNA-seq data from eight tissues (flower bud, flower mature, capsule mature, leaf upper, leaf middle, leaf lower, root and stem) and aimed at producing a functional coverage across the tissues. In addition, to compare <i>N. rustica</i> with its progenitors using transcriptomes generated under similar conditions, a second transcriptome was generated using three tissues at two time points under two different growing conditions. Comparable samples were generated for all three putative progenitors. The samples included roots and lower and upper leaves from pre-flowering plants grown in the greenhouse, roots and lower and upper leaves from pre-flowering plants grown in the field, and roots and lower and upper leaves and flowers from flowering plants grown in the greenhouse (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#MOESM6">6</a>: Figure S5). Total RNA was extracted using the RNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen, Hilden, Germany).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec33">Sequencing and bioinformatics</h4><p>Libraries were generated using an Illumina TruSeq Stranded Kit and were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2500. Reads were demultiplexed, and Trimmomatic (version 0.32) was used to remove Illumina adapters and trailing bases with a quality cutoff of 10, trim the reads and retain only paired reads with a minimum length of 50 bp. The reads were aligned using HISAT2 (<a href="https://ccb.jhu.edu/software/hisat2/index.shtml">https://ccb.jhu.edu/software/hisat2/index.shtml</a>, version 2.0.1 beta). Aligned reads were filtered to include only reads flagged as PAIRED and to exclude reads flagged as SECONDARY, QCFAIL or SUPPLEMENTARY. Reads overshooting the scaffolds were removed by a custom script. For each individual sample and tissue, the mapped reads were assembled into transcripts, and these sample-wise transcript sets were then merged into a final set of transcripts using Cuffmerge (<a href="http://cole-trapnell-lab.github.io/cufflinks/">http://cole-trapnell-lab.github.io/cufflinks</a>). Putative peptides were extracted by identifying the longest open reading frame in the transcript (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#MOESM2">2</a>: Tables S5–S6).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec34">Expressed gene family clustering with OrthoMCL</h3><p>Peptides sequences from <i>N. rustica</i>, its three putative progenitors and those of <i>N. tabacum</i> TN90 were clustered to determine the structures of the gene families using the OrthoMCL software (<a href="http://orthomcl.org">http://orthomcl.org</a>). For each species, transcriptomes assembled using comparable read sets were used (three tissues/three condition set). Datasets were filtered and queried using a BLAST algorithm according to OrthoMCL requirements. OrthoMCL scripts were run, and the output was compiled into a Venn diagram using the gplots library for R.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec35">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>OrthoMCL was further run using the sets described above and comparable proteomes from <i>N. sylvestris</i> and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i>, as well as tomato (ITAG 2.3, reference) as an outgroup. Core gene groups were chosen from the OrthoMCL clustering based on the criterion that each group should have two representative proteins in the cluster from the allotetraploids <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. tabacum</i>, and one representative from the remaining diploid species. Of the 12,401 ortholog groups, approximately half fulfilled this strict ortholog-only criterion (6315). For these clusters, the putative peptides were extracted and aligned using Muscle (<a href="http://www.drive5.com/muscle/">http://www.drive5.com/muscle</a>) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Edgar RC. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 2004;32:1792–7." href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d16228e10492">42</a>]. The alignments were trimmed to exclude 3′ and 5′ gapped regions, resulting in only the core consensus being included. From this set, all of the alignments were removed, of which more than 5% of the column had gaps in any one of the sequences, resulting in 3160 alignments. The alignments were used to generate a protein sequence-based phylogeny and estimate K<sub>a</sub>/K<sub>s</sub> rates.</p><p>
                  <i>Inferring N. knightiana/N. paniculata ancestry based on shared ortholog protein groups.</i>
                </p><p>For the above clusters, phylogenetic trees were built, and the list of clusters was further filtered depending on the presence of a clear clustering in the cladogram of the gene variants to their ancestors. For example, there needs to be one <i>N. tabacum</i> protein corresponding to one <i>N. sylvestris</i>, one <i>N. tabacum</i> protein clustering with one <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> protein, one <i>N. rustica</i> protein with one <i>N. undulata</i> protein and one <i>N. rustica</i> clustering closest to <i>N. knightiana</i>/<i>N. paniculata</i>. Using the clustering information, the closest neighbor was determined for the this <i>N. rustica</i> protein. This could be <i>N. knightiana</i>, <i>N. paniculata</i> or the common ancestor of both. The number of occurrences of each assignment was counted.</p></div></div></section>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="https://doi.org/10.1111%2Fj.1469-8137.2005.01480.x" aria-label="View reference 11">Article</a>

                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar1">Funding</h3>
                <p>Philip Morris Products S.A. was the sole source of funding for this project. No other grants from funding agencies in the public, commercial, or not-for-profit sectors were received.</p>
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar2">Availability of data and materials</h3>
                <p>Sequence data for <i>Nicotiana rustica</i> have been deposited in the DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank nucleotide core database at BioProject PRJNA335601. The assembled genome has been deposited with the accession number MDKG00000000. Illumina reads are available under accession numbers SRR8173839-SRR8173851 (DNA-seq) and SRR8169773-SRR8169836 (RNA-seq).</p>
                <p>Sequence data for <i>Nicotiana undulata</i> have been deposited in the DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank nucleotide core database at BioProject PRJNA335602. The assembled genome has been deposited with the accession number MDKH00000000. Illumina reads are available under accession numbers SRR8173251-SRR8173255 (DNA-seq) and SRR8169524-SRR8169558 (RNA-seq).</p>
                <p>Sequence data for <i>Nicotiana paniculata</i> have been deposited in the DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank nucleotide core database at BioProject PRJNA335603. The assembled genome has been deposited with the accession number MDKI00000000. Illumina reads are available under accession numbers SRR8173256-SRR8173261 (DNA-seq) and SRR8169598-SRR8169634 (RNA-seq).</p>
                <p>Sequence data for <i>Nicotiana knightiana</i> have been deposited in the DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank nucleotide core database at BioProject PRJNA335604. The assembled genome has been deposited with the accession number MDKJ00000000. Illumina reads are available under accession numbers SRR8169719-SRR8169728 (DNA-seq) and SRR8169559-SRR8169597 (RNA-seq).</p>
                <p>Protein sequences of the genes and key regulators of the alkaloid pathway are available in Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s12864-018-5241-5#MOESM7">7</a>: File S1.</p>

HB, HL, AV provided plant material and LB, VL and SG contributed to the biological interpretation of the results. SO and JT prepared the sequencing libraries and performed the sequencing. GL performed the elemental analysis and the LC-UV/MS analysis. NS and JNDB carried out genome and transcriptome analysis. NS, JNDB, LB, VL and NVI wrote the manuscript. NVI supervised the study. All authors read and approved the final manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding author</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to

                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar3">Ethics approval and consent to participate</h3>
                <p><i>N. rustica</i> L. var. Brasilia No. 7 (PI 499174, TR13, USDA–GRIN database), <i>N. paniculata</i> L. (PI 555545, TW 99, USDA–GRIN database), <i>N. knightiana</i> Goodsp. (PI 555527, TW 73, USDA–GRIN database), <i>N. undulata</i> Ruiz &amp; Pav. (PI 555575, TW 146, USDA–GRIN database), <i>N. tabacum</i> L. cv. TN90 (PI 543792, TC 586, USDA–GRIN database), <i>N. sylvestris</i> Speg. &amp; Comes (PI 555569, TW 136, USDA–GRIN database) and <i>N. tomentosiformis</i> Goodsp. (PI 555572, TW 142, USDA–GRIN database) were used in the experiments. Dedicated field studies were conducted in accordance with local legislation.</p>

We observed 336 SNPs unique to <i>N. undulata</i>, 8 shared between <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. undulata</i>, 303 SNPs shared by <i>N. rustica</i>, <i>N. paniculata</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i>, 7 by <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. paniculata</i>, 17 by <i>N. rustica</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i> and 11 by <i>N. paniculata</i> and <i>N. knightiana</i>. (PNG 76 kb)</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM5"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-018-5241-5/MediaObjects/12864_2018_5241_MOESM5_ESM.pdf" data-supp-info-image="">Additional file 5:</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>Figure S4.</b> Phylogenic tree of the putrescine methyltransferase (<b>a</b>), cytochrome P450 nicotine N-demethylase (<b>b</b>), and glutamine synthetase (<b>c</b>) proteins of <i>N. rustica</i>, <i>N. tabacum</i> and their respective progenitors, and their gene expressions, in FPKMs, in various tissues under different growth conditions. For each gene, the relative expressions are highlighted in shades of grey. Sequences for <i>N. tabacum</i> PMT1 (Q42963), PMT2 (Q9SEH7), PMT3 (Q9SEH5), PMT4 (Q9SEH4), CYP82E1 (Q9ZWK2), CYP82E2 (Q38Q85), CYP82E3 (Q38Q84), CYP82E4 (L7Y094), CYP82E5 (A1XEH1), CYP82E8 (A1XEM0), CYP82E10 (E5G962) were obtained from Uniprot. LL: lower leaf, UL: upper leaf, RO: root, FL: flower, GH_1: greenhouse pre-flowering, FI: field pre-flowering, and GH_2: greenhouse flowering. The numbers at each node of the tree correspond to the bootstrapping confidence level of each split. The gene names are composed of the abbreviated progenitor species name, the gene symbol, and the start and stop positions of the BLAST algorithm-based match expressed as a fraction of the protein length. (PDF 485 kb)</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM6"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-018-5241-5/MediaObjects/12864_2018_5241_MOESM6_ESM.pdf" data-supp-info-image="">Additional file 6:</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>Figure S5.</b> Tissues, number of biological replicates and species collected for transcriptome annotation and comparative gene expression analysis. (PDF 87 kb)</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item" data-test="supp-item" id="MOESM7"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="https://static-content.springer.com/esm/art%3A10.1186%2Fs12864-018-5241-5/MediaObjects/12864_2018_5241_MOESM7_ESM.txt" data-supp-info-image="">Additional file 7:</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>File S1.</b> Protein sequences of the genes and key regulators of the alkaloid pathway. (TXT 90 kb)</p></div></div></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="rightslink" data-title="Rights and permissions"><div class="c-article-section" id="rightslink-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="rightslink">Rights and permissions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="rightslink-content">
